43:2324:96856:29:29:225:387:269:121:g_d0_f9:121:g_d0_f9:C:φMθ(D)−η−ξ≥φMθ(N)+ξ,
43:2325:96876:29:19:82:529:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:which, givenξ>0, contradicts (58), establishing thatp(D)>p(N), i.e., that democracy is
43:2326:96962:19:18:82:127:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:captured.
43:2327:96971:18:19:99:530:203:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:But, by the same reasoning,p(D)>p(N)impliesf(φMθ(D)−η−ξ)>f(φMθ(N)+ξ),
43:2328:97040:19:18:82:529:184:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:thusφMθ(D)−η−ξ<φMθ(N)+ξ.SinceFis strictly monotonic, this impliesˆp(N)>ˆp(D),
43:2329:97117:18:18:82:330:166:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:establishing the Markov regime-switching structure.
43:2330:97168:18:19:99:529:148:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The equilibrium in this proposition is the richest and perhaps the most interesting one we
43:2331:97258:19:18:82:529:129:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:have encountered so far. The equilibrium still takes a Markov regime-switching structure with
43:2332:97351:18:19:82:528:111:121:g_d0_f6:121:g_d0_f3:B:fluctuations between democracy and nondemocracy; but in democracy, there is no guarantee
43:2333:97439:19:0:82:528:92:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:that economic institutions will be those favored by the citizens.  While in all the previous
44:2334:97533:0:18:82:529:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:models we studied, the elite were able to imposeboththeirpoliticaland economic wishes at
44:2335:97621:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the same time, here we have an equilibrium pattern whereby democracy persists, but the elite
44:2336:97713:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:are able to impose their favorite economic institutions. In fact, the proposition shows that
44:2337:97805:19:18:82:529:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:(given Assumption 2’) the elite will be able to impose labor repressive economic institutions
44:2338:97898:18:19:82:400:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:with ahigherprobability under democracy than in nondemocracy.
44:2339:97959:19:18:99:528:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The intuition for this (somewhat paradoxical) result is that in democracy there is an addi-
44:2340:98050:18:19:82:529:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:tional benefit for the elite to invest in de facto political power, which is to induce a switch from
44:2341:98150:19:18:82:529:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:democracy to nondemocracy. Consequently, the elite invest in their de facto power sufficiently
44:2342:98244:18:18:82:529:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:more in democracy that they are able to obtaintheir favorite economic institutions with a
44:2343:98333:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:greater probability.32Nevertheless, the elite are happierin nondemocracy, because the cost
44:2344:98423:19:18:82:529:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of investing in their de facto political power in democracy is significantly higher. In fact, it
44:2345:98519:18:19:82:529:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:is precisely because they prefer nondemocracy to democracy that they are willing to invest
44:2346:98609:19:18:82:528:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:more in their de facto political power in democracy and obtain the labor repressive economic
44:2347:98701:18:18:82:528:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:institutions with a high probability. What about citizens? If there were no additional benefit
44:2348:98795:18:19:82:529:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of democracy,ν(S=D)>0, then citizens would actually be worse offin democracy than
44:2349:98876:19:18:82:528:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in nondemocracy, because they only care about economic institutions and economic institu-
44:2350:98965:18:19:82:528:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:tions are more likely to be labor repressive in democracy than in nondemocracy. Thus when
44:2351:99054:19:18:82:529:387:121:g_d0_f9:121:g_d0_f3:B:ν(S=D)=0, citizens would never choose democracy, and would be happy to remain in non-
44:2352:99139:18:19:82:528:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:democracy (given the limited ability that they have to solve the collective action problem).
44:2353:99231:19:18:82:528:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Therefore, the ideological or other benefits of democracy encapsulated inν(S=D)>0create
44:2354:99318:18:18:82:528:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the possibility of the captured democracy equilibrium, whereby the citizens are willing to vote
44:2355:99413:18:19:82:474:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and defend democracy, but democracy at the end caters to the wishes of the elite.
44:2356:99494:19:18:99:528:295:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Therefore, this model features both state dependence in political and economic institutions,
44:2357:99586:18:19:82:528:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and also coexistence of change in political institutions with persistence in economic institutions
44:2358:99684:19:18:82:528:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:(i.e., the presence of labor repression in democracy). In fact, it is straightforward to see that
44:2359:99781:18:19:82:529:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the larger isξ, the more likely is the configuration with stable democracy choosing economic in-
44:2360:99877:19:18:82:528:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:stitutions in line with the interests of the elite. Consequently, this model with durable political
44:2361:99976:18:18:82:528:203:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:institutions and captured democracy provides both the richest set of predictions regarding the
44:2362:100070:18:19:82:528:185:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:persistence of economic and political institutions, and a potential explanation for the patterns
44:2363:100166:19:16:82:528:166:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:discussed in Section 7, which illustrate how many societies become and remain democratic,
44:2364:100255:16:15:91:529:150:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:32This result is not independent of functional form assumptions. For example, if we relax Assumption 2’, it
44:2365:100362:15:11:82:528:135:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:is possible to obtain an equilibrium with a semi-captured democracy where political institutions still follow a
44:2366:100473:11:10:82:528:124:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:Markov-switching structure with state dependence, but the probability of labor repressive economic institutions
44:2367:100584:10:11:82:529:114:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:in democracy is positive but no higher than in nondemocracy, i.e.,p(N)≥p(D)>0. Nevertheless, relaxing
44:2368:100685:11:0:82:467:103:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:Assumption 2’ does not guarantee that such an equilibrium will exist; it only makes it possible.
45:2369:100783:0:34:82:414:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:but continue to pursue policies that favor the traditional landed elite.
45:2370:100855:34:29:82:226:666:209:g_d0_f4:209:g_d0_f4:C:6Effective Reform
45:2371:100872:29:19:82:528:637:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:In this section, we briefly discuss how institutional persistence can be broken by effective
45:2372:100964:19:18:82:528:618:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:reforms. Our framework shows how the equilibrium path may feature invariance (i.e., labor
45:2373:101053:18:18:82:528:600:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:repressive and generally dysfunctional economic institutions remaining in place despite shocks
45:2374:101147:18:19:82:529:582:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:that change the political organization of society) or state dependence (where dictatorship is
45:2375:101240:19:18:82:528:563:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:more likely to follow dictatorship than it is to follow democracy). Are there any major reforms
45:2376:101335:18:19:82:328:545:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:that could break these various types of persistence?
45:2377:101387:19:18:99:529:526:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The comparative static results above suggest potential answers to this question. In par-
45:2378:101475:18:19:82:528:508:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:ticular, the results so far show that a change in political institutions from nondemocracy to
45:2379:101568:19:18:82:528:489:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:democracy is likely to be effective (in terms of leading to equilibrium competitive labor mar-
45:2380:101662:18:18:82:528:471:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:kets and persistent democracy) under two alternative (but complementary) scenarios. First,
45:2381:101752:18:19:82:529:453:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:if democracy creates a substantial advantagefor the citizens in the form of a large value ofη,
45:2382:101846:19:18:82:528:434:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:then as shown by Corollary 1 this will end the cycle of institutional persistence and make the
45:2383:101940:18:19:82:342:416:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:permanent consolidation of democracy an equilibrium.
45:2384:101992:19:18:99:529:397:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Second, one of the following reforms is undertakensimultaneouslywith the switch to democ-
45:2385:102081:18:18:82:528:379:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:racy, then the economy is less likely to switch back to nondemocracy and labor repressive eco-
45:2386:102175:18:19:82:529:361:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:nomic institutions: (1) a reduction inφDin terms of the model of subsection 4.1, so that the
45:2387:102267:19:18:82:528:342:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:traditional elites are less able to control politics in a democratic society (for example, prevent-
45:2388:102366:18:19:82:528:324:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:ing local threats of violence or the capture of political parties by the traditional elites would
45:2389:102463:19:18:82:529:305:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:achieve such an outcome). (2) a reduction in∆R, for example, by means of an increase in
45:2390:102550:18:19:82:528:287:121:g_d0_f9:121:g_d0_f3:B:A, which will reduce the potential rents that thelanded elites can obtain and will discourage
45:2391:102643:19:18:82:309:268:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:them from investing in de facto political power.
45:2392:102691:18:18:99:529:250:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:This discussion therefore illustrates that while politics as business-as-usual may favor the
45:2393:102783:18:19:82:528:232:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:elite even in democracy, undertaking simultaneous and significant reforms may change the
45:2394:102871:19:18:82:529:213:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:character of the political equilibrium, makingdemocracy and competitive labor markets more
45:2395:102961:18:19:82:529:195:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:likely. An attractive example of simultaneous reform leading to a significant change in the
45:2396:103052:19:18:82:528:176:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:distribution of political power in society is the 1688 Glorious Revolution in England, which
45:2397:103144:18:19:82:528:158:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:not only changed de jure power by dethroning the Stuart monarchy, but also by substantially
45:2398:103235:19:18:82:528:139:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:increasing the role of the Parliament and the allocation of economic resources in society, ir-
45:2399:103329:18:18:82:529:121:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:reversibly altered the distribution of de factopolitical power (see, for example, North and
45:2400:103420:18:0:82:528:103:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Weingast, 1989, Acemoglu, Johnson and Robinson, 2005a). Another interesting example of
46:2401:103508:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:simultaneous reforms arises in the next section where we describe how central aspects of the
46:2402:103600:18:18:82:529:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:economic institutions of the U.S. South, having survived the Civil War,finally changed in the
46:2403:103693:18:35:82:111:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:1960s.
46:2404:103699:35:29:82:261:629:209:g_d0_f4:209:g_d0_f4:C:7  Historical Perspective
46:2405:103724:29:18:82:528:600:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:We now examine some case studies of the persistence of power, elites and institutions from the
46:2406:103818:18:19:82:528:582:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:U.S. South, Latin America and Africa (Liberia). Our objective is neither to provide a compre-
46:2407:103911:19:18:82:529:563:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:hensive historical account nor to test our model. Instead, we wish to illustrate salient cases
46:2408:104005:18:19:82:528:545:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of coexistence of change in political institutions with persistence in the underlying economic
46:2409:104099:19:18:82:529:526:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:system, which has been the motivating empiricalpattern for our theoretical analysis. In all
46:2410:104190:18:18:82:528:508:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:cases, we will interpret the historical events through the lenses of the models presented above,
46:2411:104286:18:19:82:528:490:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:though we will also attempt to highlight potential disagreements or alternative interpretations
46:2412:104381:19:29:82:245:471:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:among historians when they exist.
46:2413:104414:29:25:82:339:442:145:g_d0_f4:145:g_d0_f4:C:7.1  Persistence of the Elites in U.S. South
46:2414:104458:25:19:110:502:417:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:“De landlord is landlord, de politician is landlord, de judge is landlord, de shurf is landlord,
46:2415:104554:19:18:110:502:398:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:ever’body is landlord, en we ain’ got nothin.” Testimony of a Mississippi sharecropper to
46:2416:104643:18:30:110:501:380:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:an official of the Agricultural Adjustment Administration in 1936, Schulman (1994, p. 16).
46:2417:104733:30:18:99:528:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:An important example which illustrates our thesis in the continuation of the economic
46:2418:104818:18:19:82:528:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:system based on labor repression, plantation and low-wage uneducated labor in the U.S. South
46:2419:104910:19:18:82:528:313:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:before and after the significant changes in political institutions brought about by the Civil
46:2420:105003:18:18:82:114:295:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:War.33
46:2421:105009:18:19:99:529:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Before the Civil War, the South was significantly poorer than the U.S. average income at
46:2422:105097:19:18:82:529:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:about 70% of GDP per-capita (e.g., Barro and Sala-i-Martin, 1992, Easterlin, 1960).34The
46:2423:105185:18:19:82:528:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:South lacked industry (Bateman and Weiss, 1981, Wright, 1986, Table 2.4, p. 27) and in 1860
46:2424:105276:19:15:82:528:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the total manufacturing output of the South was less than that of either Pennsylvania, New
46:2425:105366:15:15:91:529:206:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:33Although many features of the Southern economic system, such as the plantation system and the reliance on
46:2426:105473:15:11:82:529:191:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:low-wage labor, persisted, as Wright (1986) emphasizes, there were also important discontinuities, for example,
46:2427:105584:11:11:82:529:180:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the fact that the planter class transformed itself from “laborlords,” mostly relying on their slave property, to
46:2428:105696:11:7:82:426:169:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:“landlords,” more reminiscent to the large-scale plantation owners of South America.
46:2429:105780:7:15:91:529:162:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:34The relative poverty of the South has been hotly debated by scholars. Although Fogel and Engerman (1974)
46:2430:105886:15:11:82:528:147:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:pointed out that if the South had been an independent country in 1860 it would have been amongst the 10
46:2431:105989:11:11:82:528:136:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:richest in the world, the consensus view is that the South was relatively backward with poor institutions in 1860
46:2432:106102:11:11:82:528:125:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:(a view which in its modern form goes back at least to Genovese, 1965). To the extent that it was relatively
46:2433:106210:11:11:82:529:114:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:prosperous compared to other parts of the world at the time, this was because it had recently benefited from
46:2434:106318:11:11:82:528:103:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:a huge boom in cotton prices (Wright, 1978) and also because it was embedded in a society whose institutions
46:2435:106426:11:0:82:421:92:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:were formed in the 17th century, before the development of the plantation economy.
47:2436:106510:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:York or Massachusetts (Cobb, 1984, p.  6).  The South had very low rates of urbanization
47:2437:106598:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:(around 9% as opposed to 35% in the Northeast) and relatively little investment in infrastruc-
47:2438:106692:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:ture. For example, the density of railroads (miles of track divided by land area) was three times
47:2439:106789:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:higher in the North than in Southern states. The situation with respect to canal mileage was
47:2440:106881:18:19:82:528:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:similar (Wright, Table 2.1, p. 21). Perhaps more importantly, especially in the context of the
47:2441:106975:19:18:82:529:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:potential for future economic growth and industrialization, the South was not even innovative
47:2442:107068:18:19:82:271:590:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:for the sectors in which it specialized.35
47:2443:107110:19:18:99:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Although there is no consensus about why the South was backward, all scholars relate this
47:2444:107199:18:18:82:528:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in various ways to the planation economy and slavery.  Wright (1986) argues that because
47:2445:107287:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:slaves were a mobile asset, there was no incentive for planter interests to support investment
47:2446:107381:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in public goods such as infrastructure, and so manufacturing could not develop. Bateman and
47:2447:107472:18:19:82:528:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Weiss (1981) show that Southern planters did not invest in industry, even though the rate of
47:2448:107564:19:18:82:528:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:return was superior to that in agriculture. A plausible explanation for the lack of innovation
47:2449:107658:18:18:82:528:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:is that slavery limited the possibilities for productive investment. Slaves were forbidden to
47:2450:107751:18:19:82:529:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:own property or to become educated in most Southern states, presumably because this made
47:2451:107839:19:18:82:528:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:them easier to control. But this pattern of labor repression also condemned plantations to
47:2452:107929:18:19:82:528:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:low-skilled labor forces and possibly removed the incentives of planters to innovate. It is also
47:2453:108025:19:18:82:528:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:possible that the lack of urbanization and industry was a consequence of concerns about the
47:2454:108116:18:19:82:436:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:control of slaves and collective action, though Goldin (1976) disputes this.
47:2455:108192:19:18:99:528:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:In the aftermath of the Civil War, the income per-capita of the South fell to about 50%
47:2456:108279:18:18:82:528:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the U.S. average. If the organization of the slave economy had been the reason why the
47:2457:108368:18:19:82:528:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:South had been relatively backward in 1865, one might have imagined that the abolition of
47:2458:108457:19:18:82:529:295:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:slavery in 1865 would have removed this blockage to Southern prosperity. The evidence and
47:2459:108546:18:19:82:528:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:historical interpretations show that the abolition of slavery had a surprisingly small effect on
47:2460:108642:19:18:82:529:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the Southern economy.36Though planters initially tried and failed to reintroduce the gang
47:2461:108731:18:19:82:528:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:labor system with the freed slaves, out of the ashes of the Civil War emerged a low wage labor
47:2462:108825:19:18:82:528:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:intensive economy based on labor repression. Cut offfrom the rest of the United States, income
47:2463:108919:18:18:82:529:203:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:per-capita remained at about half the average until the 1940s when itfinally began slowly to
47:2464:109011:18:16:82:529:185:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:converge.37Just as before the Civil War, there was systematic underinvestment in education
47:2465:109101:16:15:91:529:169:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:35For example, during the period 1837 to 1859 while the average numbers of patents issued per year relating
47:2466:109208:15:7:82:477:154:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:to corn and to wheat were respectively 12 and 10, it was just 1 for cotton (see Schmookler, 1972).
47:2467:109306:7:15:91:529:147:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:36Despite this consensus, there is a debate on the related matter of why Southern incomes fell after the
47:2468:109410:15:11:82:529:132:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:Civil War. The interpretation by Fogel and Engerman(1974) is based on the idea that the slavery system was
47:2469:109516:11:7:82:471:121:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:relatively efficient, though this has been challenged, see, for example, Ransom and Sutch (2001).
47:2470:109613:7:15:91:529:114:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:37Wright (1986, p. 70) notes “the isolation of the southern unskilled labor market was a basic background
47:2471:109718:15:0:82:416:99:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:condition for virtually the whole epoch between the Civil War and World War II.”
48:2472:109800:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:(Margo, 1990).38The main incentive for this seems to have been to impede migration (see
48:2473:109887:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Wright, 1986, p. 79). In 1900 all but two of the non-Southern states had enacted compulsory
48:2474:109978:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:schooling laws, while none had such laws in the South except Kentucky (Woodward, 1951, p.
48:2475:110067:19:18:82:529:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:399). Though industrial development did beginmore systematically after 1865, Cobb (1984,
48:2476:110155:18:31:82:143:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:p. 17) notes:
48:2477:110168:31:18:110:502:596:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:“The industries that grew most rapidly in the post-Reconstruction decades were typical
48:2478:110254:18:18:110:502:578:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:of an underdeveloped economy in that they utilized both cheap labor and abundant raw
48:2479:110338:18:19:110:502:560:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:materials ... such industries hardly promised to elevate the region to economic parity with
48:2480:110429:19:30:110:211:541:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:the rest of the nation”.
48:2481:110453:30:19:99:529:511:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:So why did the economic system of the South change so little following the Civil War,
48:2482:110538:19:18:82:529:492:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:especially given the significant changes in political institutions? Atfirst, this persistence of
48:2483:110634:18:19:82:529:474:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f7:B:economic institutions appears at odds with the significant changes in the distribution ofde
48:2484:110725:19:18:82:528:455:121:g_d0_f7:121:g_d0_f3:B:jurepower that took place after the Civil War, for example, with the enfranchisement of the
48:2485:110816:18:18:82:528:437:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:freed slaves, and the repeal of the Missouri compromise, which had previously cemented the
48:2486:110906:18:19:82:344:419:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:political power of the South in the federal government.
48:2487:110961:19:18:99:529:400:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:We believe the answer is related to the forces emphasized in our model, in particular, to
48:2488:111050:18:19:82:528:382:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the exercise of de facto political power by the Southern landed elites to compensate for the
48:2489:111142:19:18:82:528:363:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:loss of their de jure political power.39Consistent with our approach, there was considerable
48:2490:111234:18:19:82:529:345:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:persistence in the identity and power of the political elites. For example, Wiener (1978) studied
48:2491:111331:19:18:82:528:326:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the persistence of the planter elite in 5 counties of the black belt of western Alabama. Tracking
48:2492:111428:18:18:82:528:308:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:families from the U.S. census and considering those with at least $10,000 of real estate, he found
48:2493:111526:18:19:82:528:290:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:that (p. 9) “of the 236 members of the planter elite in 1850, 101 remained in the elite in 1870.”
48:2494:111623:19:18:82:529:271:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Interestingly, this rate of persistence was very similar to that experienced in the antebellum
48:2495:111717:18:19:82:529:253:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:period; “of the 236 wealthiest planters families of 1850, only 110 remained in the elite a decade
48:2496:111814:19:18:82:528:234:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:later” (p.  9).  Nevertheless, “of the 25 planters with the largest landholdings in 1870, 18
48:2497:111906:18:17:82:527:216:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:(72%) had been in the elite families in 1860; 16 had been in the 1850 elite group.”40Table
48:2498:111996:17:11:91:517:199:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:38Kousser (1974, p. 17) records a post-bellum adage “To educate a ‘nigger’ is to spoil a goodfield hand.”
48:2499:112101:11:14:91:529:188:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:39Another possibility would be that the continuation of large plantation agriculture was because of its relative
48:2500:112213:14:11:82:529:174:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:economic efficiency. Yet this seems inconsistent with the available evidence. For example, Wright (1986, p.
48:2501:112320:11:11:82:528:163:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:84) argues “The plantations survived the Civil War, and their survival had little to do with their efficiency as
48:2502:112432:11:11:82:529:152:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:producing units ... cotton and tobacco could be grown just as efficiently on family-sized farms. No, the key to
48:2503:112543:11:11:82:528:141:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the survival of the plantation was the ability of the former slave owners to hold on to their land in the midst of
48:2504:112657:11:11:82:528:130:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:intense legal and political struggles after 1865. In national politics, the planters successfully blocked proposals
48:2505:112772:11:7:82:314:119:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:for land confiscation and redistribution to the freedmen.”
48:2506:112830:7:15:91:529:112:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:40Death during the Civil War appears to have had little impact on the persistence of the planter elites because
48:2507:112941:15:0:82:493:97:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the law exempted one slaveholder from military service for every 20 slaves held (Wiener, 1978, p. 18).
49:2508:113045:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:1 reproduces these data from Wiener (1978) and shows the high degree of persistence in the
49:2509:113135:18:18:82:252:682:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:identity of Southern landed elites.41
49:2510:113172:18:19:99:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:After the end of the Civil War, more or less the same group of planter elites controlled the
49:2511:113264:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:land and used various instruments to re-exert their control over the labor force. Though the
49:2512:113356:18:19:82:529:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:specific economic institution of slavery did not persist, the evidence shows a clear line of per-
49:2513:113453:19:18:82:528:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:sistence in the economic system of the South based on plantation-type agriculture with cheap
49:2514:113545:18:19:82:528:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:labor. This economic system was maintained through a variety of channels, including both
49:2515:113633:19:18:82:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:control of local politics and exercise of potentially violent de facto power. As a consequence,
49:2516:113728:18:18:82:528:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in the words of W.E.B. Du Bois (1903, p. 88), the South became “simply an armed camp for
49:2517:113816:18:19:82:198:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:intimidatingblack folk.”
49:2518:113840:19:18:99:529:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The planter elite successfully staffed or co-opted the members of the Freedmen’s Bureau,
49:2519:113928:18:19:82:529:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:whose remit was to supervise the freed slaves. In 1865 the state legislature of Alabama passed
49:2520:114022:19:18:82:528:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the Black Code, an important landmark towards the repression of black labor. Wiener (1978,
49:2521:114112:18:18:82:528:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:p. 58) describes this as: “The Black Code of Alabama included two key laws intended to assure
49:2522:114205:18:19:82:529:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the planters a reliable supply of labor–a vagrancy law, and a law against the ‘enticement’ of
49:2523:114298:19:18:82:528:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:laborers”. These laws were designed to impede labor mobility and reduce competition in the
49:2524:114388:18:19:82:156:406:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:labor market.42
49:2525:114403:19:18:99:529:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:In addition to moulding the legal system in their favor, “Planters used Klan terror to keep
49:2526:114494:18:19:82:528:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:blacks from leaving the plantation regions, to get them to work, and keep them at work, in the
49:2527:114588:19:18:82:529:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:cottonfield” (Wiener, 1978, p. 62).43In his seminal study of the politics of the South after
49:2528:114680:18:18:82:528:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:World War II, Key (1949, p. 9) sums up the pattern of persistence of the institutions of the
49:2529:114772:18:19:82:528:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:South both before and after the Civil War as the“extraordinary achievement of a relatively
49:2530:114862:19:18:82:409:295:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:small minority–the whites of the areas of heavy Negro population.”
49:2531:114928:18:19:99:529:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:A key to the persistence of the antebellum system after the Civil War was the continued
49:2532:115015:19:18:82:528:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:control over land. For example, in the debate over the redistribution of 40 acres of land to the
49:2533:115111:18:19:82:529:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:freedmen (vetoed by President Andrew Johnson in 1865), Congressman George Washington
49:2534:115195:19:16:82:300:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Julian argued (quoted in Wiener, 1978, p. 6):
49:2535:115240:16:15:91:528:205:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:41Other studiesfind similar persistence in other parts of the South. See, for example, Ransom and Sutch
49:2536:115343:15:11:82:529:190:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:(2001, pp. 78-80) on landonwership in Dallas County Alabama, Huffman (1974) on Clarke County, Georgia,
49:2537:115445:11:11:82:528:179:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:and Billings (1979) on North Carolina. This research to some extent contradicted earlier studies which had seen
49:2538:115556:11:7:82:458:168:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:far more change in the identity of Southern elites after the Civil War (e.g., Woodward, 1951)
49:2539:115649:7:15:91:529:161:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:42The extent to which southern planters were able to exert classical monopsony power is controversial, see
49:2540:115755:15:11:82:529:146:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:Alston and Kauffman (2001). Nevertheless, economic historians do agree on the use of coercion, intimidation,
49:2541:115863:11:7:82:393:135:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:violence and segregationist legislation to repress and control free black labor.
49:2542:115943:7:15:91:528:128:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:43Relatedly, Alston and Ferrie (1989) show how planters controlled their labor force by offering them “security”
49:2543:116055:15:0:82:193:113:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:from violence and lynching.
50:2544:116084:0:18:126:502:700:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:“Of what avail would be an act of congress totally abolishing slavery ...  if the old
50:2545:116169:18:30:110:344:682:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:agricultural basis of aristocratic power shall remain?”
50:2546:116224:30:19:99:528:652:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:A third strategy, again consistent with the emphasis on the de facto political power of the
50:2547:116315:19:18:82:528:633:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:elite in our theoretical analysis, was control of the local political system. Following the Civil
50:2548:116412:18:19:82:528:615:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:War, the period called ‘Reconstruction’ lasted until 1877 (see Stampp, 1965, and Foner, 1989).
50:2549:116506:19:18:82:528:596:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:In this period Republican politicians contested power in the South and, with the help of the
50:2550:116598:18:18:82:528:578:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Union Army, engineered some social changes. Nevertheless, this induced a systematic backlash
50:2551:116690:18:19:82:529:560:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in the guise of support for the Democratic Partyand the so-called ‘Redeemers.’ In 1877, in the
50:2552:116784:19:18:82:528:541:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:context of a log-roll between President Rutherford Hayes and Southern national politicians,
50:2553:116875:18:19:82:528:523:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Union soldiers were withdrawn from the South and the region left to its own devices. The period
50:2554:116970:19:18:82:528:504:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:after 1877 then marked the real recrudescence of the antebellum elite. The ‘redemption’ of the
50:2555:117064:18:19:82:529:486:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:South involved the systematic disenfranchisement of the black (and poor white) population
50:2556:117153:19:18:82:528:467:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:through the use of poll taxes and literacy tests (Key, 1949, Kousser, 1974) and the creation of
50:2557:117248:18:18:82:252:449:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:the one-party Democratic regime.44
50:2558:117282:18:19:99:528:431:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Key (1949, pp. 309-10), in his analysis of the primary elections of the Democratic party,
50:2559:117371:19:18:82:529:412:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:noted the hegemony of southern society’s “upperbrackets” and the political marginalization
50:2560:117461:18:19:82:529:394:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of its “lower brackets.” He discusses in detailthe control of North Carolina’s economic oli-
50:2561:117553:19:18:82:529:375:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:garchy over politics, noting that (p. 211): “The effectiveness of the oligarchy’s control has
50:2562:117646:18:19:82:529:357:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:been achieved through the elevation to office of persons fundamentally in harmony with its
50:2563:117736:19:18:82:137:338:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:viewpoint.”
50:2564:117747:18:18:99:529:320:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:This picture is also confirmed by the analysis of Wright (1986, p. 78), who writes “Even
50:2565:117835:18:19:82:528:302:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in the 1930s, southern representatives in Washington did not use their powerful positions to
50:2566:117927:19:18:82:528:283:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:push for new federal projects, hospitals, public works and so on. They didn’t, that is, as long
50:2567:118022:18:19:82:388:265:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:as the foundations of the low-wage regional economy persisted.”
50:2568:118085:19:18:99:529:246:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:In addition to disenfranchisement a whole gamut of segregationist legislation–the so-called
50:2569:118176:18:19:82:528:228:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Jim Crow laws–was enacted (Woodward, 1955, for the classic analysis). These laws turned the
50:2570:118267:19:18:82:529:209:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:postbellum South into an effective “apartheid” society where blacks and whites lived different
50:2571:118361:18:18:82:529:191:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:lives. As in South Africa, these laws were aimedat controlling the black population and its
50:2572:118452:18:16:82:144:173:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:labor supply.
50:2573:118465:16:15:91:529:157:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:44Key (1949, pp. 8-9), for example, notes: “Two-party competition would have been fatal to the status of
50:2574:118569:15:11:82:528:142:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:black-belt whites. It would have meant in the ’nineties an appeal to the Negro vote and it would have meant
50:2575:118676:11:11:82:528:131:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:... Negro rule in some black-belt counties. From another standpoint, two-party competition would have meant
50:2576:118783:11:11:82:528:120:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the destruction of southern solidarity in national politics ... Unity on the national scene was essential in order
50:2577:118897:11:11:82:528:109:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:that the largest possible bloc could be mobilized to resistany national move towards interference with southern
50:2578:119008:11:0:82:340:98:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:authority to deal with the race question as was locally desired.”
51:2579:119075:0:18:99:529:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Consequently, the South entered the 20th century as a primarily rural society. “It remained
51:2580:119166:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:an agrarian society with a backward technology that still employed hand labor and mule power
51:2581:119258:18:19:82:529:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:virtually unassisted by mechanical implements” Ransom and Sutch (2001 pp. 175-176).45In
51:2582:119345:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:1900, the South’s urbanization rate was 13.5%, as compared to 60% in the Northeast (Cobb,
51:2583:119434:18:19:82:143:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:1984, p. 25).
51:2584:119447:19:18:99:529:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Ransom and Sutch’s (2001, p. 186) assessment of the implications of this economic and
51:2585:119532:18:19:82:528:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:political system in the South for economic progress is representative of the consensus view:
51:2586:119624:19:18:82:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:“Southerners erected an economic system that failed to reward individual initiative on the
51:2587:119714:18:18:82:529:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:part of blacks and was therefore ill-suited to their economic advancement. As a result, the
51:2588:119805:18:19:82:529:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:inequities originally inherited from slavery persisted. But there was a by-product of this effort
51:2589:119902:19:29:82:424:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:at racial repression, the system tended to cripple all economic growth”:
51:2590:119974:29:18:126:501:487:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:“When whites used threats of violence to keep blacks from gaining an education, prac-
51:2591:120059:18:19:110:502:469:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:ticing a trade, or purchasing land, they systematically prevented blacks from following
51:2592:120146:19:18:110:502:450:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:the three routes most commonly travelled by other Americans in their quest for self-
51:2593:120230:18:19:110:502:432:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:advancement.  With over half the population held in ignorance and forced to work as
51:2594:120313:19:18:110:502:413:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:agricultural laborers, it is no wonder that the South was poor, underdeveloped, and with-
51:2595:120402:18:29:110:400:395:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:out signs of economic progress” Ransom and Sutch (2001, p. 177).
51:2596:120466:29:19:99:528:366:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:All in all, the Southern equilibrium, based on the exercise of de facto power by the landed
51:2597:120557:19:18:82:528:347:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:elite, plantation agriculture and low-wage, uneducated labor, persisted well into the 20th cen-
51:2598:120652:18:19:82:528:329:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:tury, and only started to crumble after World War II. Interestingly, it was only after the demise
51:2599:120749:19:18:82:528:310:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of this Southern equilibrium, that the South started its process of rapid convergence to the
51:2600:120841:18:19:82:113:292:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:North.
51:2601:120847:19:18:99:528:273:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:What caused the collapse of the Southern equilibrium? Consistent with the emphasis in
51:2602:120932:18:18:82:528:255:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Section 6, it seems that this collapse was brought about by the juxtaposition of a variety
51:2603:121022:18:19:82:529:237:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of factors.  Initially, the depth of the GreatDepression weakened the resolve of Southern
51:2604:121111:19:18:82:529:218:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:politicians to block federal policies that might integrate the Southern economy into the rest
51:2605:121204:18:19:82:528:200:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the nation (Schulman, 1994). Other important factors include the great outmigration and
51:2606:121294:19:18:82:528:181:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the impact of the simultaneous mechanization of cotton picking on the labor requirements of
51:2607:121385:18:18:82:529:163:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:agriculture. As blacks became harder to trap in the South, they also became less necessary to
51:2608:121478:18:16:82:161:145:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:generate rents.46
51:2609:121495:16:15:91:529:129:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:45See Whatley (1985) for a hypothesis explaining the lack of innovation in the post-bellum South in terms of
51:2610:121603:15:7:82:305:114:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the poor incentives created by sharecropping contracts.
51:2611:121658:7:15:91:529:107:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:46An interesting question, which is not central to our interpretation, is whether mechanization of cotton
51:2612:121763:15:0:82:528:92:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:reduced the cost of black migration to the Southern landed elite, who then tolerated such outmigration, or
52:2613:121871:0:18:99:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Wright’s (1999, Table 1, p. 281) data on net migration from the South shows the large
52:2614:121956:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:increase in black outmigration starting in the 1940s and Heinicke (1994, Table 1, p. 506) shows
52:2615:122051:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the concurrent rapid spread of mechanized cotton picking. First introduced in 1949, by the end
52:2616:122145:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the 1950s mechanical cotton picking was the rule rather than the exception in the South;
52:2617:122236:18:19:82:422:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:half of the crop in the key cotton states was being picked mechanically.
52:2618:122308:19:18:99:529:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:In addition to these changes, the Southern equilibrium was disrupted by the collective action
52:2619:122401:18:19:82:528:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the Civil Rights movement and a sequence of Supreme Court and government decisions, such
52:2620:122492:19:18:82:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:as Brown versus Board of Education in 1954, the Civil Rights Act of 1964 and the Voting Rights
52:2621:122586:18:18:82:529:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Act of 1965. These significant events can be interpreted as a decline in the ability of the landed
52:2622:122684:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:elite to exercise de facto political power in democracy. McAdam’s (1983, Figures 1 and 2, p.
52:2623:122776:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:739) data on the number of civil rights collective actions (bus boycotts and sit-ins) shows a
52:2624:122869:18:19:82:528:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:sharp increase starting in 1960 (see also Chong, 1991), while Wright (1999, Figure 2, p. 273)
52:2625:122962:19:18:82:528:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:records the dramatic increase in the registration of black voters in Southern states over this
52:2626:123056:18:18:82:529:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:period. It thus became increasingly more difficult for the Southern landed elites to control
52:2627:123148:18:19:82:528:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the political process (see also the detailed analysis of the impact of the Voting Rights Act on
52:2628:123243:19:29:82:405:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:economic growth in the South in Besley, Persson and Sturm, 2005).
52:2629:123308:29:25:82:495:395:145:g_d0_f4:145:g_d0_f4:C:7.2  Persistence of Elites and Economic Institutions in Latin America
52:2630:123377:25:19:82:529:370:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Despite the significant changes in political institutionsthat have taken place since the colonial
52:2631:123474:19:18:82:529:351:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:era in Latin America, the capture of political and economic institutions by elites is an enduring
52:2632:123571:18:19:82:528:333:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:theme of much of the historical literature onthe sub-continent. There are numerous studies
52:2633:123661:19:18:82:528:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:documenting both the capture of democratic politics by traditional elites and the resulting
52:2634:123752:18:19:82:528:296:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:continuation of labor repressive policies in agriculture in many Central and Latin American
52:2635:123843:19:18:82:528:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:countries. Some notable examples include Zeitlin (1984) and Zeitlin and Ratcliff(1988) on
52:2636:123932:18:18:82:528:259:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Chile, Smith (1979) on Mexico, and Paige (1997) on Central America. A central question for
52:2637:124022:18:19:82:529:241:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:understanding the economic development (or lack thereof) in Central and Latin America and
52:2638:124111:19:18:82:528:222:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the Caribbean is therefore how and why the economic system based on labor repression and
52:2639:124199:18:19:82:529:204:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:cheap labor in agriculture failed to change following the political reforms that have gradually
52:2640:124294:19:18:82:462:185:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:brought more democratic means of collective decision-making in these societies.
52:2641:124373:18:19:99:529:167:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:As in the U. S. South, the answer is related to the exercise of de facto political power
52:2642:124461:19:18:82:528:148:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:by traditional elites in the context of democratic politics. The capture of democratic politics
52:2643:124556:18:18:82:528:130:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in Colombia, for example, took many different forms, depending on the specific conditions
52:2644:124645:18:20:82:528:112:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and the options open to the traditional elites, with the most common forms being the actual
52:2645:124736:20:0:82:514:92:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:whether the changes in the supply of cheap labor causedby black outmigration encouraged mechanization.
53:2646:124840:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:domination of political parties by the elites, electoral fraud, blocking of entry of new parties,
53:2647:124937:18:18:82:175:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and use of violence.
53:2648:124957:18:19:99:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The most obvious form of political capture is the actual running of political parties and
53:2649:125046:19:18:82:529:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:offices by members of the landed elite. This has been particularly the case in much of Central
53:2650:125140:18:19:82:529:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:America and has been carefully documented byStone (1975, 1990). His genealogical research
53:2651:125229:19:18:82:528:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:shows the extraordinary extent to which elites have persisted in Costa Rica and other Central
53:2652:125322:18:19:82:291:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:American countries from the colonial times.
53:2653:125365:19:18:99:529:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Figures 1 and 2 (from Stone, 1990) show the striking number of presidents since indepen-
53:2654:125453:18:18:82:528:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:dence in various Central American countries that are related to two conquistadors, Cristóbal
53:2655:125545:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:de Alfaro and Juan Vázquez de Coronado, who both arrived in Central America in the 16th
53:2656:125632:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:century.  Figure 1 shows that no less that 48 presidents were direct descendents of Alfaro,
53:2657:125723:18:19:82:528:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:25 of them in Costa Rica. Alfaro’s descendents also include the Somoza dynasty who ruled
53:2658:125811:19:18:82:529:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Nicaragua for most of the 20th century until theSandinista Revolution in 1979. Figure 2 shows
53:2659:125904:18:18:82:528:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the 29 presidents descended from Vázquez, including again the Somoza clan and many of the
53:2660:125993:18:19:82:312:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:same presidents of Costa Rica related to Alfaro.
53:2661:126041:19:18:99:529:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:As a case study we now examine Colombia in more detail. Colombia became independent
53:2662:126124:18:19:82:529:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:from Spain in 1819 and the Liberal and Conservative partiesfirst competed in the election
53:2663:126213:19:18:82:529:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of 1850.47These two parties have maintained their hold on political power ever since. As
53:2664:126301:18:19:82:529:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:early as 1856 the Liberals introduced universal male suffrage and as many as 40% of adult
53:2665:126390:19:18:82:529:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:males voted (Bushnell, 1971). After the 1863 Constitution, voting rights were determined at
53:2666:126481:18:18:82:529:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the state level, but several of them maintained universal suffrage (see Delpar, 1981). In the
53:2667:126574:18:19:82:528:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:19th century, the parties contested elections, but they also fought for power. Although the
53:2668:126665:19:18:82:528:295:121:g_d0_f6:121:g_d0_f3:B:fighting sometimes took the form of civil wars, the parties also frequently reached collusive
53:2669:126758:18:19:82:528:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:agreements to make sure that other political parties, especially those representing peasants
53:2670:126850:19:18:82:529:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and workers, were unable to enter the politicalsystem. For example, after the civil war from
53:2671:126942:18:19:82:528:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:1899 to 1902, the two parties introduced a power-sharing agreement, which divided all the
53:2672:127031:19:18:82:528:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:seats in the legislature between the two parties by means of the ‘incomplete vote’ (Mazzuca
53:2673:127122:18:18:82:528:203:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and Robinson, 2004). This system lasted until 1929. After a civil war in the 1950s (a period
53:2674:127214:18:19:82:529:185:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:know asLa Violencia), the parties resurrected this collusive agreement in 1958, splitting the
53:2675:127307:19:18:82:529:166:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:legislature 50-50 and also agreeing to take turns at the presidency for 16 years. In practice
53:2676:127400:18:16:82:408:148:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:this power sharing agreement endured until the middle of the 1980s.
53:2677:127467:16:15:91:529:132:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:47The best studies of the origins of the parties are Colmenares (1968), Safford (1972) and Delpar (1981).
53:2678:127572:15:11:82:529:117:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:Some trace the alignments to factions that formed in the late 1820s over whether or not Simón Bolívar should
53:2679:127680:11:0:82:529:106:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:be made Emperor, though there have also typically been significant policy differences between these two parties.
54:2680:127794:0:18:99:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The Liberals and Conservatives managed to successfully block entry of other parties to
54:2681:127880:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the political system through a variety of strategies. Even after the reintroduction of universal
54:2682:127976:18:19:82:529:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:suffrage in 1936, fraud, violence, and engineering the electoral rules to create barriers to entry
54:2683:128074:19:18:82:529:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:were widespread.48Electoral fraud is, in fact, a recurrent theme in Colombian politics and took
54:2684:128169:18:19:82:529:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:many forms (see, for example, Bushnell, 1993, and Chaves, Fergusson and Robinson, 2005).
54:2685:128257:19:18:82:528:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Fraud was also used by the traditional parties to directly derail populist challenges, such as
54:2686:128351:18:19:82:443:590:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:during the attempt by Rojas Pinilla to win the 1970 presidential election.49
54:2687:128427:19:18:99:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Violence has also been endemic in Colombian politics. The potential “Perón” of Colom-
54:2688:128512:18:18:82:529:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:bia, Jorge Eliécer Gaitán, who was likely to pursue populist and redistributive policies, was
54:2689:128605:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:murdered in 1948 just when it seemed likely he would become the leader of the Liberal party.
54:2690:128697:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Luis Carlos Galán was murdered in 1990 just as he was emerging as the leader of the Liberal
54:2691:128788:18:19:82:529:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:party.50In the 1980s thefirst alternative political party to seriously emerge since the beginning
54:2692:128885:19:18:82:529:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the National Front in 1958 was the Unión Patriótica. This party was effectively obliterated
54:2693:128979:18:18:82:508:461:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:in the 1980s by the systematic murder of its candidates (see, for example, Kline, 1999).51
54:2694:129069:18:19:99:529:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Finally, as in Central America, the political parties in Colombia have featured strong
54:2695:129155:19:18:82:529:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:family ties. The Conservative Ospina family has provided three Presidents, Mariano Ospina
54:2696:129244:18:19:82:528:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Rodríguez (1857-1861), Pedro Nel Ospina (1922-1926) and Mariano Ospina Pérez (1946-1950).
54:2697:129333:19:18:82:528:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:The Liberal López family provided not just Alfonso between 1934 and 1938, and again between
54:2698:129424:18:16:82:528:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:1942 and 1945, but also Alfonso López Michelsen between 1974 and 1978. The Liberal Lleras
54:2699:129513:16:15:91:529:353:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:48This is related to the absence of a socialist party in Colombia.  Some scholars have suggested that the
54:2700:129618:15:11:82:529:338:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:relatively egalitarian frontier expansion that took place in the second half of the 19th century has contributed
54:2701:129730:11:11:82:528:327:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:to the creation of a middle class of property owners, which checked radicalism in Colombian politics.  This
54:2702:129837:11:11:82:528:316:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:thesis does not appear to be very convincing in view of the fact that Colombia has one of the most unequal
54:2703:129943:11:7:82:515:305:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:distributions of land ownership and income in the world (on this Antioqueño expansion see Christie, 1978).
54:2704:130049:7:15:91:529:298:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:49Another factor that appears to have contributed to the continuation of the two-party system is the way
54:2705:130153:15:11:82:528:283:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the electoral system created incentives for dissidentsto stay within the traditional parties. For example, the
54:2706:130263:11:11:82:528:272:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:electoral system ensured that candidates could be elected with far fewer votes than the electoral quotient, thus
54:2707:130375:11:11:82:529:261:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:potentially guaranteeing that elite factions would get representation. At the same time, the traditional parties
54:2708:130487:11:10:82:528:250:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:allowed “dissident lists” to run, providing a platform to potential dissidents. In line with these ideas, Dix (1967,
54:2709:130603:10:11:82:528:240:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:p. 250) notes “proportional representation may have helped to a degree to reinforce the two-party system by
54:2710:130710:11:11:82:528:229:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:allowing dissident factions to gain representation according to their strength in the electorate while still not
54:2711:130822:11:11:82:529:218:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:forcing them from the party. Retaining the party label, or some version of it, they have usually been reabsorbed
54:2712:130934:11:8:82:289:207:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:into the officialist ranks after one or two elections.”
54:2713:130989:8:14:91:529:199:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:50Political entrepreneurs, like Gaitán and subsequently Alfonso López Michelsen and Galán, considered form-
54:2714:131096:14:11:82:528:185:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:ing a third party, but then always returned to the fold because the electoral system made success by third
54:2715:131202:11:11:82:529:174:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:parties difficult. Indeed, it is quite likely that the assassination of Gaitán was precisely because he hadfinally
54:2716:131316:11:7:82:528:163:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:emerged as the leader of the Liberal party, not because political rivals anticipated the creation of a third party.
54:2717:131431:7:15:91:529:156:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:51In the 1986 elections, Unión Patriótica obtained 5 seats in the Senate and 9 in the Chamber of Representa-
54:2718:131539:15:11:82:529:141:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:tives at the national level, and 14 deputies, 351 councilmen and 23 municipal mayors at the local level. By 1988,
54:2719:131652:11:11:82:528:130:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the party was wiped out, however. The Amnesty International reported that more than 500 of its members,
54:2720:131755:11:11:82:529:119:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:including its previous presidential candidate, Pardo Leal, and 4 congressmen, had been assassinated, most likely
54:2721:131867:11:0:82:394:108:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:with the involvement of members of the Colombian military and government.
55:2722:131942:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:family provided presidents in 1945-1946 and 1958-1962 (Alberto Lleras Camargo) and again
55:2723:132030:18:18:82:529:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in 1966-1970 (Carlos Lleras Restrepo).  The Conservative Pastrana family provided Misael
55:2724:132118:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Pastrana between 1970 and 1974 and Andrés Pastrana between 1998 and 2002. The son of
55:2725:132202:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Conservative President Laureano Gómez (1950-1953), Álvaro was the Conservative challenger
55:2726:132291:18:19:82:528:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in the late 1980s and early 1990s and the father of Liberal President Julio Turbay (1978-1982)
55:2727:132385:19:18:82:398:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:had previously been the most powerfulfigure in the Liberal party.
55:2728:132450:18:19:99:528:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Overall, through a variety of means, including elite domination of politics, electoral fraud,
55:2729:132543:19:18:82:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:blocking of the creation of new parties and violence, traditional elites seem to have been able
55:2730:132638:18:18:82:529:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:to have a disproportionate effect in the democratic politics of Central America and Colombia.
55:2731:132731:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:We conjecture that this pattern of elite dominance has been an important element in the
55:2732:132818:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:continuation of the economic system based on cheap labor and quasi-labor repression in the
55:2733:132908:18:29:82:269:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:countryside of many of these countries.
55:2734:132947:29:26:82:447:469:145:g_d0_f4:145:g_d0_f4:C:7.3  Persistence of Elites and Economic Institutions in Africa
55:2735:133009:26:18:82:529:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:The issues we study here are of importance outsideof the Americas, and here we illustrate them
55:2736:133103:18:19:82:529:425:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:with the example of Liberia. Modern Liberia was started in 1820 by the American Colonization
55:2737:133195:19:18:82:529:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Society (ACS) as a home for freed and repatriated African slaves.52The ACS bought land
55:2738:133281:18:19:82:528:388:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:from local chiefs around the site of the modern capital of Monrovia (named after the then
55:2739:133370:19:18:82:528:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:President of the United States James Monroe), and in 1847 Liberia became independent of the
55:2740:133461:18:18:82:528:351:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:ACS. The year 1877 saw the emergence of the True Whig Party (TWP), which would dominate
55:2741:133548:18:19:82:528:333:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:politics until the coup of Samuel Doe in 1980. The TWP comprised of the descendents of the
55:2742:133638:19:18:82:528:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:repatriated slaves, the Americo-Liberians, who dominated the party and the country for over a
55:2743:133731:18:19:82:528:296:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:century. This group set themselves up as an elite over the ‘tribal peoples’ and ran the country
55:2744:133826:19:18:82:514:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:as if it were a colony. By the 1960s Americo-Liberians were about 3-5% of the population.
55:2745:133915:18:19:99:529:259:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Despite the early emergence of the Americo-Liberian elite in Liberia, the political system
55:2746:134005:19:18:82:528:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:has relied on elections since the 19th century, and became fully democratic starting in 1944.
55:2747:134098:18:18:82:528:222:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Nevertheless, like their Central American or Southern U.S. counterparts, the Americo-Liberian
55:2748:134191:18:19:82:529:204:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:elite have been able to dominate democratic politics through a variety of means, including
55:2749:134281:19:18:82:528:185:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:extreme electoral fraud and violence, and theirdomination of politics has been a major cause
55:2750:134373:18:19:82:328:167:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the continued economic backwardness of Liberia.
55:2751:134423:19:18:99:528:148:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Thefirst study of the economic backwardness of Liberia was undertaken by a team of U.S.
55:2752:134510:18:17:82:528:130:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:based economists from Northwestern University hired by USAID in the early 1960s (Clower et
55:2753:134600:17:0:91:396:113:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:52This discussion draws on the general history of Liberia by Sawyer (1992).
56:2754:134677:0:18:82:529:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:al., 1966). Dalton (1965, p. 581) sums up theirfindings by concluding that Liberia was ruled
56:2755:134769:18:30:82:355:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:by the elite of the TWP and Americo-Liberians and that
56:2756:134823:30:19:110:502:652:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:“...the economic backwardness of Liberia is not attributable to the lack of resources or to
56:2757:134914:19:18:110:501:633:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:domination by foreignfinancial or political interests. The underlying difficulty is rather
56:2758:135004:18:18:110:501:615:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:that the traditional Americo-Liberian rulers, who fear losing political control to the tribal
56:2759:135097:18:19:110:502:597:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:people, have not allowed those changes to take place which are necessary to develop the
56:2760:135184:19:18:110:502:578:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:national society and economy. ... Like the Portuguese in Angola or the Afrikaners in South
56:2761:135274:18:19:110:502:560:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:Africa, the rulers of Liberia are the descendents of an alien minority of colonial settlers.
56:2762:135366:19:30:110:230:541:100:g_d0_f3:100:g_d0_f3:F:Americo-Liberian families.”
56:2763:135393:30:18:99:529:511:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The TWP used indirect rule adapted from theBritish to control the hinterland and ma-
56:2764:135477:18:19:82:529:493:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:nipulate chiefships. As Clower et al. record, Liberia became a two class society and different
56:2765:135571:19:18:82:528:474:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:laws, access to education and the legal system governed Americo-Liberians and tribal peoples.
56:2766:135664:18:19:82:528:456:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Dalton (1965, p. 584) points out that “Ironically, it is the ethic of Mississippi that most nearly
56:2767:135762:19:18:82:528:437:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:characterizes their outlook: to retain power in traditional fashion and keep the natives in their
56:2768:135859:18:18:82:115:419:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:place.”
56:2769:135866:18:19:99:528:401:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Before 1944, the hinterland had no political representation at all, and even after it did so,
56:2770:135959:19:18:82:528:382:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the TWP were able to cling to power through their complete control of the economy, and more
56:2771:136050:18:19:82:377:364:121:g_d0_f3:65:g_d0_f3:B:significantly, through intimidation, corruption and coercion.53
56:2772:136113:19:18:99:528:345:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The resulting economic institutions in Liberiacertainly approximated the labor repressive
56:2773:136202:18:19:82:528:327:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:institutions in the model discussed above. In the 1960s tribal peoples were still subject to
56:2774:136294:19:18:82:528:308:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:forced labor on public works and rubber plantations (1/4 of the labor force in early 1960s)
56:2775:136385:18:18:82:528:290:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and the economy was either completely controlled by the TWP or by foreigners. The state
56:2776:136472:18:19:82:528:272:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:apparatus became a huge patronage machine dedicated to promoting the interests of the TWP
56:2777:136561:19:18:82:212:253:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and the Americo-Liberians.
56:2778:136587:18:19:99:529:235:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:A key feature of Liberian politics, facilitating the exercise of power by the TWP, was the
56:2779:136677:19:18:82:528:216:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:kinship networks of the Americo-Liberians (see Fraenkel, 1964). Dalton (p. 589) argues that “to
56:2780:136772:18:19:82:529:198:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:understand Liberian politics, knowledge of kinship connections is more useful than knowledge
56:2781:136864:19:18:82:529:179:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of the Liberian constitution.” Figure 3 shows how higher political offices were monopolized by
56:2782:136958:18:18:82:528:161:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the kinship network of President Tubman, who ruled between 1944 and 1971 and was from
56:2783:137043:18:14:82:351:143:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:one of the established families of the Americo-Liberians.
56:2784:137100:14:15:91:529:129:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:53Indeed Liberia has the sad reputation as having had the most corrupt election ever held for Charles B.
56:2785:137204:15:11:82:529:114:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:King’s election in 1927. The Guinness Book of Records (1982) qualified the elections as the most fraudulent ever
56:2786:137316:11:11:82:529:103:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:reported in world history. Suffrage was constitutionally limited to some 15,000 citizens, all Americo-Liberians,
56:2787:137428:11:0:82:462:92:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:but according to the official election results some 240,000 votes were cast for Charles B. King.
57:2788:137526:0:18:99:529:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:After his defeat in the 1927 presidential elections, Thomas Faulkner accused the President-
57:2789:137617:18:18:82:529:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:elect, Charles D.B. King, of allowing slavery to exist in the Republic and that certain highly
57:2790:137711:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:placed government officials were engaged in the forced shipping of laborers to the Spanish
57:2791:137801:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:island of Fernando Po. Faulkner’s accusations spurred a wave of international reactions, and a
57:2792:137895:18:19:82:528:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Committee of the League of Nations, established to examine the allegations, indeed concluded
57:2793:137987:19:18:82:528:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:that shipment of laborers to Fernando Po and Gabon was associated with slavery because the
57:2794:138077:18:19:82:529:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:method of recruiting carried compulsion with it. Moreover, persons holding official positions
57:2795:138170:19:18:82:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:were shown to have illegally misused their office in recruiting labor with the aid of the Liberian
57:2796:138268:18:18:82:529:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Frontier Force (the national military controlled by the TWP). Not only had Americo-Liberian
57:2797:138359:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:government officials benefited from forced labour, but so had the most major foreign company
57:2798:138451:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:in Liberia, Firestone.54In essence the TWP had offered Harvey Firestone a guaranteed cheap
57:2799:138541:18:19:82:228:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:labor supply through coercion.
57:2800:138571:19:18:99:529:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:There can be few better historical examples of an elite than the Americo-Liberians and
57:2801:138657:18:18:82:528:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the TWP. Initially, they were able to restrict de jure power only to themselves, completely
57:2802:138748:18:19:82:528:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:disenfranchising the indigenous Africans. They were also able to dominate in their use of de
57:2803:138840:19:18:82:528:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:facto power because they had the support of the United States and superior weapons. Over
57:2804:138928:18:19:82:529:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:time the political system opened. After 1944 the TWP had to deal with the political inclusion
57:2805:139021:19:18:82:528:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:of peoples in the hinterland. They were nonetheless able to use their de facto power to maintain
57:2806:139117:18:19:82:268:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the economic institutions they favored.
57:2807:139156:19:18:99:529:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:Therefore, the domination of politics by theAmerico-Liberian elite and their choices of
57:2808:139243:18:18:82:529:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:(highly inefficient) economic institutions to redistribute resources to themselves gives another
57:2809:139339:18:19:82:529:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:example of the successful exercise of de facto power by an elite both under nondemocratic and
57:2810:139432:19:34:82:237:295:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:democratic political institutions.
57:2811:139466:34:29:82:206:261:209:g_d0_f4:209:g_d0_f4:C:8Conclusions
57:2812:139478:29:19:82:529:232:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Almost all theoretical and empirical research in political economy starts with the presumption
57:2813:139572:19:18:82:529:213:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:that institutions, once in place, persist and shape the political-economic interactions of different
57:2814:139672:18:19:82:528:195:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:groups and agents. Nevertheless, many societies experience frequent changes in their political
57:2815:139766:19:18:82:528:176:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:institutions, such as the end of the colonial era and the creation of the republics throughout
57:2816:139860:18:16:82:528:158:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the 19th and 20th centuries, or multiple switches between dictatorship and democracy during
57:2817:139951:16:15:91:529:142:36:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:54Vast rubber plantations were started by Firestone in 1926. Firestone obtained a one million acre concession
57:2818:140060:15:11:82:529:127:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:for a 99-year period (roughly corresponding to 10% of what was considered arable land in Liberia), was granted
57:2819:140170:11:11:82:528:116:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:the exclusive rights upon the lands selected, and became–with small, exceptions–exempted of all present and
57:2820:140277:11:0:82:133:105:82:g_d0_f3:82:g_d0_f3:F:future taxes.
58:2821:140292:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the 20th century. Certain economic institutions also change, with slavery, forced labor, and the
58:2822:140388:18:18:82:528:682:121:g_d0_f7:121:g_d0_f3:B:encomiendacoming to an end in Latin America and Caribbean, and waves of nationalization
58:2823:140475:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and privatization of industry in many less developed countries. In the face of this picture of
58:2824:140569:19:18:82:499:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:frequently changing (political and economic) institutions, do institutions really persist?
58:2825:140659:18:19:99:528:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:This paper has argued that the answer is yes, in the sense that the broad economic in-
58:2826:140745:19:18:82:529:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:stitutions, which are essential for economic outcomes, can persist even when specific political
58:2827:140840:18:19:82:528:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:institutions, or even specific economic institutions, change. In line with this perspective, it has
58:2828:140939:19:18:82:487:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:proposed a simple model of the coexistence of change and persistence in institutions.
58:2829:141024:18:18:99:528:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The basic idea is that economic institutions are decided by groups or individuals that pos-
58:2830:141115:18:19:82:528:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:sess more political power. Political power, in turn, consists of de jure power regulated by formal
58:2831:141213:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:political institutions and de facto political power, which comes from the ability of various dif-
58:2832:141310:18:19:82:529:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:ferent social groups to solve their collective action problems, lobby or bribe politicians, capture
58:2833:141409:19:18:82:528:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:and control political parties, or use paramilitaries or other means of repression. Changes in
58:2834:141502:18:18:82:528:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:specific political institutions, for example a change from nondemocracy to democracy, affects
58:2835:141595:18:19:82:528:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the distribution of de jure political power. This, however, may induce cohesive (and small)
58:2836:141686:19:18:82:528:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:groups, such as (landed) elites to increase their investments in de facto political power to offset
58:2837:141785:18:19:82:251:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:their loss of de jure political power.
58:2838:141823:19:18:99:529:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:To illustrate this mechanism in its starkest form, our baseline model assumed that the
58:2839:141909:18:19:82:528:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:technology of generating de facto political power for the elite is the same in democracy and
58:2840:142001:19:18:82:528:350:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:nondemocracy, and demonstrated the possibility of invariance, that is, a pattern where the
58:2841:142091:18:18:82:528:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:equilibrium distribution of economic institutions is independent of whether the society is de-
58:2842:142185:18:19:82:528:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:mocratic or not–despite the fact that democracy creates a real shift in the distribution of de
58:2843:142279:19:18:82:528:295:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:jure power towards the citizens. The reason for this stark result is that the landed elite invest
58:2844:142376:18:19:82:529:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:enough to fully offset their loss of de jure power when society switches from nondemocracy to
58:2845:142469:19:18:82:135:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:democracy.
58:2846:142479:18:19:99:529:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The result that investment in the de facto power by the elite entirely offsets changes in
58:2847:142568:19:18:82:528:221:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:de jure power is special, and the analysis showed how, more generally, the equilibrium takes
58:2848:142660:18:18:82:528:203:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the form of a Markov regime-switching process with state dependence. This Markov regime-
58:2849:142748:18:19:82:528:185:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:switching structure emerges when some of the assumptions of the baseline model are relaxed,
58:2850:142839:19:18:82:529:166:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:for example, when democracy is allowed to place limits on the exercise of de facto power by
58:2851:142930:18:19:82:528:148:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the elite (e.g., on their capture of political parties or use of paramilitaries), or when there are
58:2852:143029:19:18:82:351:129:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:costs of changing economic institutions in the short run.
58:2853:143086:18:19:99:528:111:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The richest model is presented in Section 5, and allows for the feature that political insti-
58:2854:143179:19:0:82:528:92:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:tutions are more difficult to change than are economic institutions. Under this assumption,
59:2855:143272:0:18:82:528:700:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:a pattern of “captured democracy” arises, whereby democracy endures, but the elite are able
59:2856:143363:18:18:82:529:682:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:to have a disproportionate effect on equilibrium economic institutions. In fact, in this model,
59:2857:143458:18:19:82:528:664:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:paradoxically, the probability of labor repressive economic institutions in agriculture may be
59:2858:143552:19:18:82:528:645:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:higher in democracy than in nondemocracy. Thismodel is useful for interpreting the historical
59:2859:143645:18:19:82:528:627:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:patterns discussed in Section 7. These patterns show the coexistence of change and persis-
59:2860:143735:19:18:82:529:608:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:tence in institutions in the U.S. South, Latin America and Africa, and how traditional elites
59:2861:143828:18:19:82:528:590:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:may be able to control democratic politics and maintain their favorite economic institutions.
59:2862:143921:19:18:82:528:571:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:In all the cases we discussed, changes in some important dimensions of political institutions,
59:2863:144015:18:18:82:528:553:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:which may have been potentially quite costly for the elites, were neutralized by the exercise of
59:2864:144111:18:19:82:529:535:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:their de facto power, for example, control of the party system or key offices, or local violence.
59:2865:144208:19:18:82:528:516:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:These examples suggest that the mechanisms highlighted by our model may be important in
59:2866:144295:18:19:82:528:498:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:understanding how frequent changes in specific political institutions go hand-in-hand with the
59:2867:144389:19:18:82:529:479:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:persistence of economic systems, with their broad distributional and efficiency characteristics
59:2868:144484:18:18:82:136:461:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:unchanged.
59:2869:144494:18:19:99:528:443:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:A:The mechanism proposed in this paper, where the identity of the elites as well as the
59:2870:144579:19:18:82:528:424:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:economic systems persist, is only one facet of institutional persistence. Another pattern, which
59:2871:144675:18:19:82:528:406:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:appears salient in practice, for example, in cases such as Bolivia, Zimbabwe, Kenya and Ghana,
59:2872:144769:19:18:82:528:387:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:is one in which the identity of the elites changes, but new elites adopt policies in line with
59:2873:144863:18:19:82:529:369:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f7:B:the worst practices of their predecessors. This is reminiscent of Michels’ (1911)Iron Law of
59:2874:144955:19:18:82:528:350:121:g_d0_f7:121:g_d0_f3:B:Oligarchy, as well as of the emphasis by sociologists such as Mills (1956), Mosca (1939) and
59:2875:145047:18:18:82:528:332:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:Pareto (1968) on the persistence and creation of elites. This pattern seems like a challenging
59:2876:145141:18:19:82:529:314:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:but important area of study. Most ambitiously, future research may strive towards a unified
59:2877:145232:19:18:82:529:295:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:model that can explain the composition of elites, when existing elites persist, when elites change
59:2878:145330:18:19:82:528:277:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:but institutions persist, and when institutions truly change. Alternatively, a model isolating
59:2879:145424:19:18:82:528:258:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the iron law of oligarchy may be useful in understanding why dysfunctional incentives persist in
59:2880:145520:18:0:82:528:240:121:g_d0_f3:121:g_d0_f3:B:the political arena, a direction we are currently pursuing in Acemoglu and Robinson (2006b).
